\section{Enlarged Residual System on the Exact Four-Class Basis}

\section{Goal}

At the corrected residual stage, the correct reduced object is the projected residual map
\[
G(\delta)=
\begin{pmatrix}
G_A(\delta)\\
G_E(\delta)\\
G_O(\delta)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\delta=
\begin{pmatrix}
a\\ e\\ o
\end{pmatrix},
\]
not a naive reduced free-energy stationary equation.

The frozen operational point is
\[
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad
\delta_f:=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)^T.
\]

The exact four-class quartic shell data are
\[
U_d=2493.1087739398513,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{opp}}=14874.171297437068,
\qquad
U_\perp=9027.17290917827,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{obl}}=2244.8894311890813.
\]

The purpose of this section is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item to write the enlarged residual system directly on the four-class basis;
\item to prove that a coarse \(A/E\)-symmetric completion cannot explain the corrected branch;
\item to identify the representation type of the first admissible microscopic completion.
\end{enumerate}

\section{1. Exact coarse \((A,E,O)\) quartic residual baseline}

Define
\[
r(\delta):=
\begin{pmatrix}
a^2\\ e^2\\ o^2
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
D(\delta):=\operatorname{diag}(a,e,o).
\]

The exact coarse four-class quartic matrix is
\[
M^{(4c)}=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2} & U_\perp & U_{\mathrm{obl}}\\[1mm]
U_\perp & \frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2} & U_{\mathrm{obl}}\\[1mm]
U_{\mathrm{obl}} & U_{\mathrm{obl}} & \frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}+2U_\perp+4U_{\mathrm{obl}}}{8}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Numerically,
\[
M^{(4c)}=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

The exact quartic residual baseline is then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(4)}(\delta)=D(\delta)\,M^{(4c)}\,r(\delta).
}
\label{eq:G4_exact_residual}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, in components,
\begin{align}
G_A^{(4)}(\delta)
&=
a\Bigl[
\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2}\,a^2
+
U_\perp e^2
+
U_{\mathrm{obl}} o^2
\Bigr],
\\[1mm]
G_E^{(4)}(\delta)
&=
e\Bigl[
U_\perp a^2
+
\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2}\,e^2
+
U_{\mathrm{obl}} o^2
\Bigr],
\\[1mm]
G_O^{(4)}(\delta)
&=
o\Bigl[
U_{\mathrm{obl}}(a^2+e^2)
+
\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}+2U_\perp+4U_{\mathrm{obl}}}{8}\,o^2
\Bigr].
\end{align}

At the corrected seed
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)^T,
\qquad
r_f=(9.216\times10^{-5},\,8.2944\times10^{-4},\,9.216\times10^{-5})^T,
\]
one obtains
\[
Q_f:=M^{(4c)}r_f
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8.494671573456258\\
8.241391656489691\\
2.580391735022780
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(4)}(\delta_f)=D(\delta_f)Q_f=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\label{eq:G4_at_seed}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Why the free-energy route stays dead}

The corresponding coarse stationary equations are obtained by dividing by
\(a,e,o\neq0\):
\begin{align}
0&=
\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2}\,a^2+U_\perp e^2+U_{\mathrm{obl}}o^2+\cdots,
\\
0&=
U_\perp a^2+\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2}\,e^2+U_{\mathrm{obl}}o^2+\cdots,
\\
0&=
U_{\mathrm{obl}}(a^2+e^2)+\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}+2U_\perp+4U_{\mathrm{obl}}}{8}\,o^2+\cdots .
\end{align}

But the already-extracted exact four-class algebraic stationary solution has
\[
r_A^{\rm crit}>0,
\qquad
r_E^{\rm crit}<0,
\qquad
r_O^{\rm crit}<0,
\]
so the free-energy stationary reconstruction is physically inadmissible.
Hence the correct next object remains the residual map itself:
\[
\boxed{
G(\delta)=0
\quad\text{must be handled directly, not through }r\text{-stationarity.}
}
\]

\section{3. Sharp coarse impossibility of an \(A/E\)-symmetric local completion}

The four-class quartic matrix is exactly invariant under
\[
A\leftrightarrow E.
\]
Assume, for contradiction, that the local completion at coarse level also preserves
\(A/E\)-symmetry and contributes only equally to the \(A\)- and \(E\)-channels at linear order.
Then the corrected-seed stationarity conditions for the \(A\)- and \(E\)-channels have identical nonquartic parts, so after division by \(a\neq0\) and \(e\neq0\) and subtraction one gets
\begin{equation}
\Bigl(\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2}-U_\perp\Bigr)(a^2-e^2)=0.
\label{eq:AE_sym_constraint}
\end{equation}

Now
\[
\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2}
=
8683.640035688459,
\qquad
U_\perp
=
9027.17290917827,
\]
so
\[
\frac{U_d+U_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2}-U_\perp
=
-343.532873489811\neq0.
\]
Therefore \eqref{eq:AE_sym_constraint} forces
\[
a^2=e^2.
\]

But at the corrected seed,
\[
a^2=(0.0096)^2=9.216\times10^{-5},
\qquad
e^2=(0.0288)^2=8.2944\times10^{-4}=9a^2.
\]
Hence
\[
a^2\neq e^2,
\]
a contradiction.

Therefore:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{a coarse }A/E\text{-symmetric local completion cannot explain the corrected branch.}
}
\end{equation}

So a nonzero \(A/E\) splitting is already necessary at the local reduced level.

\section{4. Exact quartic Jacobian and why the residual map is locally well-conditioned}

Differentiating \eqref{eq:G4_exact_residual} gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{(4)}(\delta)
=
\operatorname{diag}\!\bigl(M^{(4c)}r(\delta)\bigr)
+
2D(\delta)\,M^{(4c)}\,D(\delta).
}
\label{eq:J4_formula}
\end{equation}

At the corrected seed this becomes
\[
J^{(4)}(\delta_f)=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665531859216 & 0.4137780199567714\\
-4.991665531859216 & 22.646508448870943 & -1.2413340598703142\\
0.4137780199567714 & -1.2413340598703142 & 3.603395005929136
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with eigenvalues
\[
3.52129589,\qquad 8.35219818,\qquad 24.47164948.
\]

So although the quartic coefficient matrix itself is indefinite, the quartic
\emph{residual Jacobian} at the corrected seed is already positive definite.
This proves that the local residual map is well-conditioned even though the naive
free-energy reconstruction is not.

\section{5. Direct enlarged residual system}

The correct enlarged residual system is not
\[
G(\delta)=G^{(4)}(\delta)
\]
but rather
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)=G^{(4)}(\delta)+\mathcal N(\delta),
}
\label{eq:enlarged_residual_system}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\mathcal N(\delta)
\]
collects everything not captured by the exact four-class quartic baseline:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal N(\delta)
=
G(\delta)-D(\delta)M^{(4c)}r(\delta).
}
\label{eq:N_def}
\end{equation}

At the frozen corrected seed,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal N(\delta_f)=G(\delta_f)-G^{(4)}(\delta_f).
}
\label{eq:N_seed}
\end{equation}
Since
\[
\|G(\delta_f)\|\approx 0.0379185,
\qquad
\|G^{(4)}(\delta_f)\|\approx 0.2521901,
\]
one necessarily has
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\mathcal N(\delta_f)\|
\ge
0.2142716098179067.
}
\label{eq:N_lower_bound}
\end{equation}

Thus the nonquartic remainder is not perturbatively tiny at the corrected branch.

\section{6. First admissible local completion: symmetric non-diagonal linear remainder}

The simplest local residual completion is to approximate
\[
\mathcal N(\delta)\approx S\,\delta
\]
near the corrected seed, with \(S=S^T\) a real symmetric \(3\times3\) matrix.
Then the local completed residual model is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_{\mathrm{loc}}(\delta)=D(\delta)M^{(4c)}r(\delta)+S\delta.
}
\label{eq:Gloc_model}
\end{equation}

If one asks only for exact stationarity at the frozen corrected seed, then the condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S\delta_f=-G^{(4)}(\delta_f).
}
\label{eq:S_constraint}
\end{equation}

Among all symmetric matrices satisfying \eqref{eq:S_constraint}, the minimum-Frobenius solution is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S_{\min}
=
\frac{yx^T+xy^T}{\|x\|^2}
-
\frac{x^Ty}{\|x\|^4}\,xx^T,
\qquad
x:=\delta_f,\quad
y:=-G^{(4)}(\delta_f).
}
\label{eq:Smin_formula}
\end{equation}

Evaluating this gives
\[
S_{\min}=
\begin{pmatrix}
-0.839960218174805 & 2.450804313533534 & -0.302298414680853\\
2.450804313533534 & -7.145183917627953 & 0.837818903051675\\
-0.302298414680853 & 0.837818903051675 & 0.235363388813100
\end{pmatrix},
\]
which indeed satisfies
\[
S_{\min}\delta_f=-G^{(4)}(\delta_f).
\]

This is the first viable corrected residual completion already found numerically.

\section{7. Why diagonal-only completion is impossible}

If one restricts to diagonal completion only,
\[
S=\operatorname{diag}(s_A,s_E,s_O),
\]
then \eqref{eq:S_constraint} forces
\[
s_A=-Q_{f,A},
\qquad
s_E=-Q_{f,E},
\qquad
s_O=-Q_{f,O},
\]
that is,
\[
s_A=-8.494671573456258,
\qquad
s_E=-8.241391656489691,
\qquad
s_O=-2.580391735022780.
\]

But then
\[
J^{(4)}(\delta_f)+S
\]
has a negative eigenvalue. Therefore diagonal-only completion cannot produce an admissible local corrected residual model.

So one obtains the sharp statement:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the first admissible local completion must be genuinely non-diagonal.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{8. Honest microscopic interpretation}

What has been identified at theorem level is not yet the unique microscopic operator,
but the \emph{representation type} of the missing completion.

The missing microscopic source must satisfy all of the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item it must contribute directly to the residual map, not merely to a reconstructed free energy;
\item it must break the coarse \(A/E\) symmetry locally;
\item it cannot be purely diagonal in \((A,E,O)\);
\item its dominant mixing channel must involve the \(A/E\) sector.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the first microscopic completion beyond the exact four-class quartic baseline must be an orbit-mixing residual operator of schematic type
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal N_{\mathrm{mic}}:\ (A,E,O)\longrightarrow(A,E,O),
\qquad
\mathcal N_{\mathrm{mic}}\not\propto \operatorname{diag}.
}
\end{equation}

At the present stage, the exact microscopic parent of this operator is still open.
What is already fixed is that any candidate microscopic source must reduce, after projection,
to a local \(3\times3\) residual operator with nonzero \(A/E\) splitting and genuine non-diagonal mixing.

\section{9. Final local mainline statement}

Therefore the corrected residual quartic closure program has now reached the following sharp form:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)=D(\delta)M^{(4c)}r(\delta)+\mathcal N(\delta),
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal N(\delta_f)\neq 0,
\qquad
\|\mathcal N(\delta_f)\|\ge 0.2142716098179067,
}
\end{equation}
and the first admissible local completion requires
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{non-diagonal }A/E\text{-breaking orbit mixing.}
}
\end{equation}

So the quartic basis problem is solved, but the true remaining frontier is now exactly:
\[
\boxed{
\text{identify the microscopic origin of the projected non-diagonal residual operator } \mathcal N_{\mathrm{mic}}.
}
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The corrected branch cannot be explained by the failed \((u,v,\kappa)\) kernel, and even the exact four-class quartic enlargement
\[
U_{\alpha\beta}^{(4c)}
\]
does not close the branch by itself.

The correct reduced object is the projected residual map
\[
G(\delta),
\]
and its exact quartic baseline is
\[
G^{(4)}(\delta)=D(\delta)M^{(4c)}r(\delta).
\]

At the frozen corrected seed
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)^T,
\]
the quartic residual Jacobian
\[
J^{(4)}(\delta_f)
=
\operatorname{diag}(M^{(4c)}r_f)+2D_fM^{(4c)}D_f
\]
is positive definite, but the quartic baseline itself overshoots strongly:
\[
\|G(\delta_f)-G^{(4)}(\delta_f)\|\ge 0.2142716098179067.
\]

Therefore the missing completion is not a tiny correction.
It must be a genuinely residual, non-diagonal, \(A/E\)-breaking orbit-mixing operator.

A coarse \(A/E\)-symmetric completion is impossible, because it would force
\[
a^2=e^2,
\]
whereas the corrected seed has
\[
e^2=9a^2.
\]

Diagonal-only completion is also impossible, because it leaves a negative eigenvalue in the completed Jacobian.

Hence the first admissible local corrected completion must already be non-diagonal, and the remaining mainline problem is to identify its microscopic projected origin.

\section{Jacobian-Level Mainline Beyond the Exact Four-Class Quartic Baseline}

\section{Goal}

The corrected residual quartic mainline has already fixed the following data:
\[
\delta_f:=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)^T,
\]
\[
G_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix},
\]
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with
\[
\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959>0.
\]

The exact remaining local object is not the shell means
\[
(\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O),
\]
but the full corrected residual Jacobian
\[
\boxed{
J_f:=DG(\delta_f),
\qquad
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\]

The exact executable next extraction is the centered-difference audit
\[
\boxed{
J_{\cdot Y}
\approx
\frac{
G(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_Y)-G(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_Y)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
\qquad
Y\in\{A,E,O\},
}
\]
and, if desired, the Richardson upgrade
\[
\boxed{
\widetilde J_f^{\mathrm{Rich}}
=
\frac{4\widetilde J_f^{[\varepsilon/2]}-\widetilde J_f^{[\varepsilon]}}{3}
=
J_f+O(\varepsilon^4).
}
\]

Thus the next exact problem is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{extract }J_f,\text{ compute }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)},\text{ and classify }R_f.
}
\]

\section{1. Geometry-adapted basis at the corrected seed}

The corrected seed has the exact shape
\[
\delta_f=0.0096(1,-3,1)^T.
\]
Hence its norm is
\[
\rho:=\|\delta_f\|
=
0.0096\sqrt{11}
\approx
0.03183959798741184.
\]

Define the normalized tangent direction
\[
t:=\frac{\delta_f}{\|\delta_f\|}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1)^T.
\]

Choose two orthonormal transverse directions
\[
n_1:=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1)^T,
\qquad
n_2:=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3)^T,
\]
so that
\[
B:=(t\,|\,n_1\,|\,n_2)\in O(3).
\]

Then
\[
B^T\delta_f
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\rho\\0\\0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This is the correct local basis for the corrected-branch Jacobian audit, because it separates:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the branch-tangent direction \(t\),
\item one \(A/O\)-antisymmetric transverse direction \(n_1\),
\item one complementary coarse transverse direction \(n_2\).
\end{enumerate}

\section{2. Exact quartic baseline in the adapted basis}

Transform the known quartic Jacobian:
\[
J_{f,B}^{(4)}:=B^TJ_f^{(4)}B.
\]
Direct evaluation gives
\[
J_{f,B}^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
23.24934224 & 3.78278610 & -3.12859268\\
3.78278610 & 6.43553954 & 1.80527992\\
-3.12859268 & 1.80527992 & 6.66026178
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

So even before extracting the full Jacobian, the quartic baseline already shows:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the tangent direction }t\text{ is the stiffest local direction of the quartic residual geometry.}
}
\end{equation}

Moreover, the quartic baseline already couples \(t\) to both transverse directions:
\[
(J_{f,B}^{(4)})_{t n_1}\neq 0,
\qquad
(J_{f,B}^{(4)})_{t n_2}\neq 0.
\]

\section{3. Exact minimum-Frobenius completion and its adapted-basis structure}

The first viable local corrected completion already known in the files is the minimum-Frobenius symmetric matrix
\[
S_{\min}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-0.839960218174805 & 2.450804313533534 & -0.302298414680853\\
2.450804313533534 & -7.145183917627953 & 0.837818903051675\\
-0.302298414680853 & 0.837818903051675 & 0.235363388813100
\end{pmatrix},
\]
satisfying
\[
S_{\min}\delta_f=-G_f^{(4)}.
\]

Let
\[
y:=-G_f^{(4)}.
\]
Expand \(y\) in the adapted basis:
\[
y=y_t\,t+y_1\,n_1+y_2\,n_2,
\qquad
\begin{pmatrix}
y_t\\y_1\\y_2
\end{pmatrix}
=
B^Ty
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-0.24674990\\
-0.04014746\\
0.03320438
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Because \(x:=\delta_f=\rho t\), the exact minimum-Frobenius formula
\[
S_{\min}
=
\frac{yx^T+xy^T}{\|x\|^2}
-
\frac{x^Ty}{\|x\|^4}xx^T
\]
simplifies drastically. In the adapted basis one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(S_{\min})_B
=
\begin{pmatrix}
y_t/\rho & y_1/\rho & y_2/\rho\\
y_1/\rho & 0 & 0\\
y_2/\rho & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\label{eq:Smin_adapted_exact}
\end{equation}

Numerically,
\[
(S_{\min})_B
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-7.74978075 & -1.26092870 & 1.04286422\\
-1.26092870 & 0 & 0\\
1.04286422 & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This is the key local geometric result.

\subsection{Interpretation}

Equation \eqref{eq:Smin_adapted_exact} proves that the first admissible local completion has the following structure:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{no pure transverse-transverse correction at all;}
}
\end{equation}
\[
(S_{\min})_{n_1n_1}=(S_{\min})_{n_1n_2}=(S_{\min})_{n_2n_2}=0.
\]

Instead, the entire completion is of the form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S_{\min}
=
\frac{y_t}{\rho}\,tt^T
+
\frac{y_1}{\rho}(tn_1^T+n_1t^T)
+
\frac{y_2}{\rho}(tn_2^T+n_2t^T).
}
\label{eq:Smin_rank2_form}
\end{equation}

Therefore the first viable non-diagonal completion is not a renormalization of the transverse \(2\times2\) block itself.
It is a \emph{tangent-coupling completion}.

\section{4. Completed local prior Jacobian}

If one uses the minimum-Frobenius completion as the best local prior, the completed Jacobian is
\[
J_f^{\mathrm{prior}}
:=
J_f^{(4)}+S_{\min}.
\]

In the adapted basis:
\[
J_{f,B}^{\mathrm{prior}}
=
J_{f,B}^{(4)}+(S_{\min})_B
=
\begin{pmatrix}
15.49956149 & 2.52185740 & -2.08572845\\
2.52185740 & 6.43553954 & 1.80527992\\
-2.08572845 & 1.80527992 & 6.66026178
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Its eigenvalues are
\[
3.82469902,\qquad 8.35243128,\qquad 16.41823251,
\]
so it remains positive definite.

Thus the first admissible completion does two things and only two things:

\begin{enumerate}
\item it reduces the tangent stiffness
\[
23.24934224\ \longrightarrow\ 15.49956149,
\]
\item it weakens the tangent-to-transverse couplings,
while leaving the pure transverse block unchanged.
\end{enumerate}

\section{5. Consequence for the microscopic origin problem}

This already sharpens the microscopic target considerably.

A diagonal-only correction would correspond, in the adapted basis, to adding diagonal terms to the transverse block as well.
But the first viable completion does not do that.

So the missing microscopic source is not naturally interpreted as a simple orbitwise self-energy
\[
A\mapsto q_A A,\qquad E\mapsto q_E E,\qquad O\mapsto q_O O.
\]

Instead, the observed local pattern points to a projected operator whose dominant coarse effect is

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{branch-tangent coupling }t\leftrightarrow n_1,\ t\leftrightarrow n_2,
\text{ with negligible intrinsic transverse renormalization at first order.}
}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, the microscopic origin should be sought among projected residual operators of the schematic form
\[
\mathcal N_{\mathrm{mic}}
\sim
\Pi_t\mathcal N\Pi_t
+
\Pi_t\mathcal N\Pi_\perp
+
\Pi_\perp\mathcal N\Pi_t,
\qquad
\Pi_\perp\mathcal N\Pi_\perp\approx 0
\]
at the first admissible local level.

\section{6. Exact extraction protocol for the real Jacobian remainder}

The prior analysis above is only a local geometric inference from the already-known viable completion.
The actual next theorem-level audit still requires the real full Jacobian:
\[
J_f=DG(\delta_f).
\]

Once \(J_f\) is extracted, define
\[
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]
Then split
\[
R_f^{(s)}:=\frac12(R_f+R_f^T),
\qquad
R_f^{(a)}:=\frac12(R_f-R_f^T).
\]

In the adapted basis,
\[
R_{f,B}:=B^TR_fB
=
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{tt} & r_{t1} & r_{t2}\\
r_{1t} & r_{11} & r_{12}\\
r_{2t} & r_{21} & r_{22}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This gives the exact classification tree:

\subsection*{Case I: small remainder}
If
\[
\|R_f\|_2\ll \lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959,
\]
then the quartic baseline controls the local Jacobian.

\subsection*{Case II: tangent-coupling remainder}
If
\[
r_{11},r_{12},r_{22}\approx 0
\]
but
\[
(r_{tt},r_{t1},r_{t2})\neq 0,
\]
then the real microscopic source matches the \(S_{\min}\)-type tangent-coupling pattern.

\subsection*{Case III: coarse full symmetric remainder}
If
\[
R_f^{(a)}\approx 0
\]
but the transverse block
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{11} & r_{12}\\
r_{21} & r_{22}
\end{pmatrix}
\]
is also large, then the next correct reduced model is a full symmetric coarse \(3\times3\) normalization layer.

\subsection*{Case IV: genuinely non-gradient local remainder}
If
\[
\|R_f^{(a)}\| \not\ll \|R_f^{(s)}\|,
\]
then the corrected reduced residual map is non-gradient already at Jacobian level.

\subsection*{Case V: coarse \((A,E,O)\) basis failure}
If the adapted-basis decomposition shows large unstable mixing that cannot be compressed into the coarse \((A,E,O)\) basis, then sign-resolved suborbit refinement becomes unavoidable.

\section{7. Sharp conclusion of the present step}

Even before the actual six-point centered-difference extraction is rerun, one theorem-level local statement is already available:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the first viable corrected residual completion is a tangent-coupling completion, not a pure diagonal or pure transverse correction.}
}
\end{equation}

So the microscopic origin problem has already narrowed to:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{find the projected microscopic residual source whose first coarse effect is }
(t\text{-}t,\ t\text{-}\perp)\text{ mixing with negligible }\perp\text{-}\perp\text{ term.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The corrected residual quartic mainline has now been sharpened from vector level to local geometry level.

The exact next theorem-level object remains
\[
J_f=DG(\delta_f),
\qquad
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\]
to be extracted by centered differences around
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)^T.
\]

However, the already-known minimum-Frobenius admissible completion \(S_{\min}\) can be analyzed exactly in the branch-adapted basis
\[
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1),
\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1),
\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3).
\]

Since
\[
\delta_f=\|\delta_f\|\,t,
\]
the completion takes the exact adapted-basis form
\[
(S_{\min})_B
=
\begin{pmatrix}
y_t/\rho & y_1/\rho & y_2/\rho\\
y_1/\rho & 0 & 0\\
y_2/\rho & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
y:=-G_f^{(4)},\quad \rho=\|\delta_f\|.
\]

Therefore the first admissible local corrected completion has no intrinsic transverse-transverse block:
\[
(S_{\min})_{\perp\perp}=0.
\]

Its only effect is:
\[
\text{tangent self-correction }+\text{ tangent-transverse mixing}.
\]

So the missing microscopic source is already constrained to be a projected tangent-coupling residual operator, not a diagonal orbitwise self-energy and not a pure transverse renormalization.

The next exact audit is therefore uniquely fixed:
\[
\boxed{
\text{extract }J_f,\ \text{compute }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)},\ \text{and test whether }R_f
\text{ really has the same tangent-coupling structure.}
}
\]

\section{Exact Local Jacobian Extraction Sheet at the Corrected Residual Stage}

\section{Short Korean explanation}

The corrected residual stage is frozen at
\[
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\]
and the primary reduced object is
\[
G(\delta)=
\bigl(G_A(\delta),G_E(\delta),G_O(\delta)\bigr)^T.
\]

The exact four-class quartic baseline is already fixed, and its Jacobian at the corrected seed is
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665531859216 & 0.4137780199567714\\
-4.991665531859216 & 22.646508448870943 & -1.2413340598703142\\
0.4137780199567714 & -1.2413340598703142 & 3.603395005929136
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with
\[
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.52129589.
\]

The next exact task is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{extract }J_f=DG(\delta_f),\qquad
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
}
\]
then compute the norm classifiers
\[
\alpha_f,\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm diag},\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm sym},\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm mix},
\]
and branch accordingly.
This is now a finite \(3\times3\) matrix audit, not a conceptual theorem-building problem.

\section{1. Exact six-point centered-difference extraction}

Let
\[
e_A=(1,0,0)^T,\qquad
e_E=(0,1,0)^T,\qquad
e_O=(0,0,1)^T.
\]
Fix a small step \(\varepsilon>0\).
For each \(Y\in\{A,E,O\}\), define
\[
G_Y^{(+)}:=G(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_Y),
\qquad
G_Y^{(-)}:=G(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_Y).
\]

Then the \(Y\)-th column of the Jacobian is extracted by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(J_f)_{\cdot Y}
\approx
\frac{G_Y^{(+)}-G_Y^{(-)}}{2\varepsilon}.
}
\label{eq:centered_column}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\[
J_f^{[\varepsilon]}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{G_A(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_A)-G_A(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_A)}{2\varepsilon}
&
\frac{G_A(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_E)-G_A(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_E)}{2\varepsilon}
&
\frac{G_A(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_O)-G_A(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_O)}{2\varepsilon}
\\[3mm]
\frac{G_E(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_A)-G_E(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_A)}{2\varepsilon}
&
\frac{G_E(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_E)-G_E(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_E)}{2\varepsilon}
&
\frac{G_E(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_O)-G_E(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_O)}{2\varepsilon}
\\[3mm]
\frac{G_O(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_A)-G_O(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_A)}{2\varepsilon}
&
\frac{G_O(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_E)-G_O(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_E)}{2\varepsilon}
&
\frac{G_O(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_O)-G_O(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_O)}{2\varepsilon}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

So the raw finite audit requires exactly six residual evaluations:
\[
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_A,\qquad
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_E,\qquad
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_O.
\]

\section{2. Richardson upgrade}

To reduce truncation error, extract both
\[
J_f^{[\varepsilon]},
\qquad
J_f^{[\varepsilon/2]}.
\]
Then define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{\rm Rich}
:=
\frac{4J_f^{[\varepsilon/2]}-J_f^{[\varepsilon]}}{3}.
}
\label{eq:richardson_J}
\end{equation}

This cancels the leading \(O(\varepsilon^2)\) centered-difference error and yields
\[
J_f^{\rm Rich}=J_f+O(\varepsilon^4).
\]

Thus the practical extraction object should be
\[
\boxed{
J_f:=J_f^{\rm Rich}.
}
\]

\paragraph{Practical note.}
Since
\[
\|\delta_f\|\approx 0.0318396,
\]
a numerically sensible first pair is
\[
\varepsilon=10^{-4},\qquad \varepsilon/2=5\times10^{-5}.
\]
This is a practical numerical choice, not a theorem-level constant.

\section{3. Exact quartic baseline and remainder}

The quartic residual is cubic-homogeneous, so
\[
J_f^{(4)}\delta_f=3G_f^{(4)}.
\]
The already fixed quartic baseline quantities are
\[
G_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix},
\]
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665531859216 & 0.4137780199567714\\
-4.991665531859216 & 22.646508448870943 & -1.2413340598703142\\
0.4137780199567714 & -1.2413340598703142 & 3.603395005929136
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with eigenvalues
\[
3.52129589,\qquad 8.35219818,\qquad 24.47164948.
\]

Now define the exact local remainders
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\qquad
E_f:=G_f-G_f^{(4)}.
}
\label{eq:Rf_Ef_defs}
\end{equation}

The vector-level theorem already shows
\[
0.8496431059127669
\le
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\le
1.1503568940872333,
\]
so strong quartic-local sufficiency is already excluded.
Therefore the only remaining question is whether the Jacobian remainder \(R_f\) is still small enough for weak quartic dominance.

\section{4. First classifier: invertibility control}

Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_f:=\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*},
\qquad
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.52129589.
}
\label{eq:alpha_f_def}
\end{equation}

Interpretation:

\[
\alpha_f<1
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
J_f
=
J_f^{(4)}+R_f
\ \text{remains invertible by a Neumann-type bound},
\]
whereas
\[
\alpha_f\ge 1
\]
means the quartic baseline no longer controls local invertibility.

Thus \(\alpha_f\) is the first hard branch discriminator.

\section{5. Branch-adapted basis}

Because
\[
\delta_f=0.0096(1,-3,1)^T,
\]
define the normalized tangent direction
\[
t:=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1)^T.
\]
Choose two orthonormal transverse directions
\[
n_1:=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1)^T,
\qquad
n_2:=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3)^T.
\]
Let
\[
B:=(t\,|\,n_1\,|\,n_2)\in O(3).
\]

Now rotate the Jacobian remainder into the branch-adapted basis:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_{f,B}:=B^TR_fB.
}
\label{eq:RfB_def}
\end{equation}

Write
\[
R_{f,B}=
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{tt} & r_{t1} & r_{t2}\\
r_{1t} & r_{11} & r_{12}\\
r_{2t} & r_{21} & r_{22}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This basis is the correct one for deciding whether the missing completion is:
\begin{enumerate}
\item diagonal coarse renormalization,
\item full symmetric coarse renormalization,
\item tangent-coupling renormalization,
\item genuinely non-gradient local structure,
\item or coarse-basis failure.
\end{enumerate}

\section{6. Exact norm decompositions}

\subsection{6.1 Diagonal coarse approximation}

Define the diagonal projection
\[
\Pi_{\rm diag}(X):=\operatorname{diag}(X_{11},X_{22},X_{33}).
\]
Then define the diagonal defect ratio
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm diag}
:=
\frac{\|R_f-\Pi_{\rm diag}(R_f)\|_F}{\max(\|R_f\|_F,\epsilon_0)}.
}
\label{eq:eps_diag_def}
\end{equation}
Here \(\epsilon_0>0\) is a tiny fixed regularizer, e.g. \(10^{-15}\).

If \(\varepsilon_{\rm diag}\) is small, a diagonal coarse quadratic layer is sufficient.

\subsection{6.2 Symmetric-gradient approximation}

Define
\[
R_f^{(s)}:=\frac12(R_f+R_f^T),
\qquad
R_f^{(a)}:=\frac12(R_f-R_f^T).
\]
Then define the antisymmetry defect ratio
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}
:=
\frac{\|R_f^{(a)}\|_F}{\max(\|R_f\|_F,\epsilon_0)}.
}
\label{eq:eps_sym_def}
\end{equation}

If \(\varepsilon_{\rm sym}\) is small, the local residual map is effectively gradient-type at Jacobian level.
If not, the map is already locally non-gradient.

\subsection{6.3 Tangent-coupling coarse model}

The first viable local completion found earlier had exactly the pattern
\[
\text{tangent self-correction }+\text{ tangent-transverse mixing},
\qquad
\perp\text{-}\perp\ \text{block negligible}.
\]
So define the tangent-coupling subspace
\[
\mathcal T
:=
\left\{
\begin{pmatrix}
x_{tt} & x_{t1} & x_{t2}\\
x_{1t} & 0 & 0\\
x_{2t} & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\right\}.
\]
Its orthogonal projection is
\[
\Pi_{\mathcal T}(R_{f,B})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{tt} & r_{t1} & r_{t2}\\
r_{1t} & 0 & 0\\
r_{2t} & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Now define the mixing defect ratio
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}
:=
\frac{\|R_{f,B}-\Pi_{\mathcal T}(R_{f,B})\|_F}{\max(\|R_f\|_F,\epsilon_0)}.
}
\label{eq:eps_mix_def}
\end{equation}

Interpretation:

\[
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}\ll 1
\]
means the missing local completion is essentially of tangent-coupling type;

\[
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}\not\ll 1
\]
means the coarse \((A,E,O)\) basis is already insufficient locally, and sign-resolved suborbit refinement is indicated.

\section{7. Exact classification rule}

Let \(\tau_{\rm cert}\in(0,1)\) be the chosen certification threshold.
Then the clean local branching rule is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\begin{array}{lll}
\alpha_f\ge 1
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{quartic baseline no longer controls local invertibility,}
\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm diag}\le \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{diagonal coarse quadratic layer,}
\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm diag}>\tau_{\rm cert},\
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}\le \tau_{\rm cert},\
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}\le \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{full symmetric tangent-coupling coarse layer,}
\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm sym}> \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{locally non-gradient residual map,}
\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm mix}> \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{coarse branch basis fails locally; move to sign-resolved suborbits.}
\end{array}
}
\label{eq:local_branch_rule}
\end{equation}

This matches the already established logical structure:
the next branch is completely determined once \(J_f\) is extracted and \(R_f\) is formed.

\section{8. Audit table to populate}

The exact computation sheet to fill is:

\[
\delta_f+\varepsilon e_A,\qquad
\delta_f-\varepsilon e_A,
\]
\[
\delta_f+\varepsilon e_E,\qquad
\delta_f-\varepsilon e_E,
\]
\[
\delta_f+\varepsilon e_O,\qquad
\delta_f-\varepsilon e_O,
\]
and the same six points again for \(\varepsilon/2\).

From these 12 residual evaluations compute:
\[
J_f^{[\varepsilon]},\qquad
J_f^{[\varepsilon/2]},\qquad
J_f^{\rm Rich},
\]
then
\[
R_f,\qquad
\alpha_f,\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm diag},\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm sym},\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}.
\]

After that, the mathematically correct next move is fixed by \eqref{eq:local_branch_rule}.

\section{9. Final consequence}

At this stage, the correct frontier is no longer another quartic basis enlargement.
The four-class quartic kernel and the coarse orbit combinatorics are already closed, and the coarse quartic sector is exactly \(A\leftrightarrow E\) symmetric.
So the missing structure must come from the local remainder:
\[
\boxed{
R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\]

The honest next theorem-level object is therefore not
\[
(u,v,\kappa),\qquad
(u_d,u_{\rm opp},u_\perp,u_{\rm obl}),
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
J_f,\quad R_f,\quad
(\alpha_f,\varepsilon_{\rm diag},\varepsilon_{\rm sym},\varepsilon_{\rm mix}).
}
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The corrected residual stage is frozen at
\[
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\]
and the primary reduced object is
\[
G(\delta)=\bigl(G_A(\delta),G_E(\delta),G_O(\delta)\bigr)^T.
\]

The exact four-class quartic baseline is already fixed, with
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665531859216 & 0.4137780199567714\\
-4.991665531859216 & 22.646508448870943 & -1.2413340598703142\\
0.4137780199567714 & -1.2413340598703142 & 3.603395005929136
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and
\[
\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.52129589.
\]

The strong quartic-sufficiency route is already excluded at vector level.
Hence the only honest remaining local question is the Jacobian remainder
\[
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]

The exact next finite audit is:

\[
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_A,\qquad
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_E,\qquad
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_O,
\]
plus the same six evaluations at \(\varepsilon/2\), followed by
\[
J_f^{\rm Rich}
=
\frac{4J_f^{[\varepsilon/2]}-J_f^{[\varepsilon]}}{3}.
\]

Then one computes
\[
\alpha_f=\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm diag},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm sym},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm mix},
\]
where \(\varepsilon_{\rm mix}\) is measured in the branch-adapted basis
\[
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1)^T,\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1)^T,\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3)^T.
\]

These four numbers determine the next mathematically correct move:

\[
\alpha_f\ge 1
\Longrightarrow
\text{quartic Jacobian no longer controls invertibility,}
\]
\[
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm diag}\ \text{small}
\Longrightarrow
\text{diagonal coarse layer,}
\]
\[
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm sym}\ \text{small},\ \varepsilon_{\rm mix}\ \text{small}
\Longrightarrow
\text{tangent-coupling symmetric coarse layer,}
\]
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}\ \text{large}
\Longrightarrow
\text{locally non-gradient residual map,}
\]
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}\ \text{large}
\Longrightarrow
\text{coarse basis failure and sign-resolved suborbit refinement.}
\]

Therefore the next exact frontier is no longer another quartic ansatz.
It is the direct finite extraction and classification of the corrected local Jacobian.

\section{Explicit Numerical Certificate from the Currently Available Admissible Local Completion}

\section{Short explanation}

At the corrected residual stage, the uploaded files do \emph{not} yet contain the actual
centered-difference Jacobian
\[
J_f:=DG(\delta_f)
\]
at the corrected seed
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
\]
So the exact solver-level certificate
\[
(\alpha_f,\varepsilon_{\rm diag},\varepsilon_{\rm sym},\varepsilon_{\rm mix})
\]
cannot yet be claimed for the true \(J_f\).

However, the files do contain the exact quartic baseline
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665531859216 & 0.4137780199567714\\
-4.991665531859216 & 22.646508448870943 & -1.2413340598703142\\
0.4137780199567714 & -1.2413340598703142 & 3.603395005929136
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and the first admissible symmetric local completion
\[
R_f^{\rm prior}:=S_{\min}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-0.839960218174805 & 2.450804313533534 & -0.302298414680853\\
2.450804313533534 & -7.145183917627953 & 0.837818903051675\\
-0.302298414680853 & 0.837818903051675 & 0.235363388813100
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Therefore the strongest honest numerical step that can be completed \emph{now} is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{compute the certificate for }R_f^{\rm prior}=S_{\min}.
}
\]

\section{1. Quartic baseline spectral data}

The eigenvalues of \(J_f^{(4)}\) are
\[
\lambda(J_f^{(4)})
=
(3.52129589,\ 8.35219818,\ 24.47164949),
\]
so
\[
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})=3.52129589.
\]

This confirms that the quartic baseline Jacobian is positive definite.

\section{2. Norms of the admissible prior remainder}

For
\[
R_f^{\rm prior}:=S_{\min},
\]
the spectral and Frobenius norms are
\[
\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_2
=
8.081109861025224,
\qquad
\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_F
=
8.087899329725035.
\]

Therefore the invertibility-control ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_f^{\rm prior}
:=
\frac{\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_2}{\lambda_*}
=
\frac{8.081109861025224}{3.52129589}
=
2.294924967594988.
}
\label{eq:alpha_prior_final}
\end{equation}

Hence
\[
\alpha_f^{\rm prior}>1.
\]

This means that the quartic baseline does \emph{not} control the completed local Jacobian by the small-remainder norm criterion.

\section{3. Diagonal-defect fraction}

Let
\[
D_f^{\rm prior}:=\operatorname{diag}(R_f^{\rm prior}).
\]
Then
\[
D_f^{\rm prior}
=
\operatorname{diag}
(-0.839960218174805,\,-7.145183917627953,\ 0.235363388813100).
\]

The diagonal-defect fraction is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm diag}^{\rm prior}
:=
\frac{\|R_f^{\rm prior}-D_f^{\rm prior}\|_F}{\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_F}
=
0.455959495840998.
}
\label{eq:eps_diag_prior_final}
\end{equation}

So about \(45.6\%\) of the prior remainder is intrinsically non-diagonal in the coarse \((A,E,O)\) basis.
Therefore a diagonal quadratic correction is not an accurate description of the admissible local completion.

\section{4. Symmetry-defect fraction}

Since \(S_{\min}\) is exactly symmetric,
\[
(R_f^{\rm prior})^T=R_f^{\rm prior},
\]
so
\[
(R_f^{\rm prior})^{(a)}
=
\frac12\left(R_f^{\rm prior}-(R_f^{\rm prior})^T\right)=0.
\]

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}^{\rm prior}
=
\frac{\|(R_f^{\rm prior})^{(a)}\|_F}{\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_F}
=
0.
}
\label{eq:eps_sym_prior_final}
\end{equation}

Thus the currently admissible local completion is perfectly gradient-type at Jacobian level.

\section{5. Adapted-basis decomposition}

Define the corrected-seed adapted orthonormal basis
\[
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1)^T,
\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1)^T,
\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3)^T.
\]
Let
\[
B=(t\,|\,n_1\,|\,n_2)\in O(3).
\]
Then
\[
\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior}:=B^T R_f^{\rm prior} B
\]
is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-7.74978075 & -1.26092870 & 1.04286422\\
-1.26092870 & 0 & 0\\
1.04286422 & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix},
}
\label{eq:Rtilde_prior_final}
\end{equation}
up to roundoff at the \(10^{-16}\) level.

Therefore the transverse \(2\times 2\) block vanishes identically:
\[
P_{\perp\perp}^{\rm prior}=0.
\]

This means that the admissible prior remainder is \emph{exactly} of tangent-coupling type:
\[
\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{tt} & * & *\\
* & 0 & 0\\
* & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\section{6. Mixing-defect fraction}

Let \(\Pi_{\mathcal T}\) denote the tangent-coupling projection in the adapted basis:
\[
\Pi_{\mathcal T}
\left(
\begin{pmatrix}
x_{tt} & x_{t1} & x_{t2}\\
x_{1t} & x_{11} & x_{12}\\
x_{2t} & x_{21} & x_{22}
\end{pmatrix}
\right)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
x_{tt} & x_{t1} & x_{t2}\\
x_{1t} & 0 & 0\\
x_{2t} & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Then
\[
\Pi_{\mathcal T}(\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior})
=
\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior},
\]
up to machine roundoff.
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}^{\rm prior}
:=
\frac{
\|\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior}-\Pi_{\mathcal T}(\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior})\|_F
}{
\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_F
}
=
9.547253466277641\times 10^{-17}.
}
\label{eq:eps_mix_prior_final}
\end{equation}

So the admissible local completion is, numerically, an \emph{exact tangent-coupling remainder}.

\section{7. Comparison with the diagonal target}

The files also give the minimal diagonal target
\[
q_A^\*=-8.494671573456258,\qquad
q_E^\*=-8.241391656489691,\qquad
q_O^\*=-2.580391735022780.
\]
Define
\[
Q^\*:=\operatorname{diag}(q_A^\*,q_E^\*,q_O^\*).
\]

Then
\[
\lambda\bigl(J_f^{(4)}+Q^\*\bigr)
=
(-0.11554365,\ 0.91059735,\ 16.23363489).
\]
So the diagonal-only completion is not admissible, because it destroys positive definiteness.

By contrast, the symmetric non-diagonal completion gives
\[
\lambda\bigl(J_f^{(4)}+S_{\min}\bigr)
=
(3.82469901,\ 8.35243128,\ 16.41823252),
\]
which is strictly positive.

\section{8. Final numerical classification available now}

Hence the strongest certificate that can honestly be completed \emph{now} is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_f^{\rm prior}=2.294924967594988,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm diag}^{\rm prior}=0.455959495840998,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}^{\rm prior}=0,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}^{\rm prior}=9.547253466277641\times10^{-17}.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the quartic baseline is not norm-dominant at local Jacobian level;
\item diagonal correction is insufficient;
\item the admissible local completion is symmetric;
\item the admissible local completion is essentially a pure tangent-coupling remainder.
\end{enumerate}

So the currently computable numerical conclusion is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the next reduced model is a symmetric non-diagonal tangent-coupling layer, not a diagonal correction.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

At the corrected residual stage, the actual centered-difference Jacobian
\[
J_f=DG(\delta_f)
\]
has not yet been supplied in the uploaded files, so the exact solver-level certificate

\alpha_f^{\rm prior}=2.294924967594988,

\section{Explicit Numerical Certificate from the Available Corrected Residual Data}

\section{1. Frozen corrected seed and exact four-class quartic baseline}

\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)^T.

The exact four-class quartic baseline Jacobian at the corrected seed is
\[
J_f^{(4)}=

so

\]

Thus the quartic baseline is positive definite.

\section{2. Available admissible local completion}

The minimum-Frobenius symmetric completion already extracted in the files is
\[
S_{\min}=
\begin{pmatrix}

2.450804313533534 & -7.145183917627953 & 0.837818903051675\\
-0.302298414680853 & 0.837818903051675 & 0.235363388813100
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

We therefore define the currently computable local remainder by
\[
R_f^{\rm prior}:=S_{\min}.
\]

\section{3. Invertibility-control ratio}

The operator and Frobenius norms of \(R_f^{\rm prior}\) are
\[
\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_2=8.081109861025224,
\qquad

\end{equation}

Therefore
\[
\alpha_f^{\rm prior}>1.
\]

So the quartic baseline is not small-remainder dominant at local Jacobian level.

\section{4. Diagonal-defect ratio}

Then

=

\end{equation}

Thus about \(45.6\%\) of the admissible prior remainder is intrinsically non-diagonal in the coarse \((A,E,O)\) basis.
So a diagonal quadratic correction is not a faithful description.

\section{5. Symmetry-defect ratio}

Since \(S_{\min}\) is exactly symmetric,
\[
(R_f^{\rm prior})^T=R_f^{\rm prior},
\]
hence its antisymmetric part vanishes:
\[
(R_f^{\rm prior})^{(a)}
=
\frac12\left(R_f^{\rm prior}-(R_f^{\rm prior})^T\right)=0.
\]

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}^{\rm prior}
:=
\frac{\|(R_f^{\rm prior})^{(a)}\|_F}{\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_F}
=
0.
}
\end{equation}

So the admissible local completion is perfectly gradient-type at Jacobian level.

\section{6. Adapted-basis decomposition}

Define the corrected-seed adapted orthonormal basis
\[
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1)^T,
\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1)^T,
\qquad

\]

is

=
\begin{pmatrix}

1.04286422 & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\]
up to machine roundoff.

Therefore the transverse \(2\times2\) block vanishes:
\[
(\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior})_{\perp\perp}=0.
\]

So the admissible prior remainder is exactly of tangent-coupling type:
\[
\widetilde R_f^{\rm prior}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{tt} & * & *\\
* & 0 & 0\\
* & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\section{7. Mixing-defect ratio}

This is numerically zero.
So the available admissible local completion is an essentially exact tangent-coupling remainder.

\section{Quantitative Newton-Mismatch Barrier at the Corrected Residual Stage}

\section{Short Korean explanation}

Even though the actual centered-difference Jacobian
\[
J_f:=DG(\delta_f)
\]
has not yet been explicitly supplied in the uploaded files, the current notes already contain a stronger result than a mere qualitative statement.

They fix:

\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\qquad
\|\delta_f\|\approx 0.03183959798741184,
\]
\[
\|G(\delta_f)\|\approx 0.03791852545979427,
\]
the exact quartic baseline Jacobian
\[
J_f^{(4)},
\qquad
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959,
\qquad
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_2\approx 24.4716494789482,
\]
and the vector-level lower bound
\[
\|E_f\|
=
\|G_f-G_f^{(4)}\|
\ge
0.21427160981790663.
\]

Moreover, the quartic Newton predictor is exactly
\[
\Delta_f^{(4)}=-\frac13\delta_f.
\]

Thus the file already gives a quantitative lower bound on the actual Newton mismatch
\[
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
:=
\frac{\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\|}{\|\delta_f\|},
\qquad
\Delta_f:=-J_f^{-1}G_f,
\]
in terms of the unknown Jacobian remainder norm \(\|R_f\|_2\), where
\[
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]

So the next sharp result is not another ansatz.
It is a numerical exclusion theorem:
if the actual Jacobian remainder lies in a strong or moderate quartic-dominance regime,
then the full Newton step must stay a definite nonzero distance away from the quartic predictor \(-\delta_f/3\).

\section{Full detailed derivation}

\section{1. Frozen corrected-branch data}

The corrected branch is frozen at
\[
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)^T.
\]
Its corrected residual norm is
\[
\|G(\delta_f)\|\approx 0.03791852545979427.
\]

The exact quartic baseline Jacobian at the corrected seed is
\[
J_f^{(4)}=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with eigenvalues
\[
\lambda_1^{(4)}\approx 3.5212959,
\qquad
\lambda_2^{(4)}\approx 8.35219818,
\qquad
\lambda_3^{(4)}\approx 24.47164948.
\]
Hence
\[
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959,
\qquad
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_2\approx 24.4716494789482.
\]

The vector remainder satisfies
\[
0.21427160981790663
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
0.2901086607374952,
\qquad
E_f:=G_f-G_f^{(4)}.
\]
In particular,
\[
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\ge
0.8496431059127669,
\]
so strong quartic sufficiency is already excluded at vector level.

\section{2. Exact quartic Newton predictor}

Because \(G^{(4)}\) is homogeneous of degree \(3\),
\[
J_f^{(4)}\delta_f=3G_f^{(4)}.
\]
Therefore the quartic-only Newton correction is exactly
\[
\Delta_f^{(4)}
:=
-\bigl(J_f^{(4)}\bigr)^{-1}G_f^{(4)}
=
-\frac13\delta_f.
\]
Hence
\[
\Delta_f^{(4)}
=
(-0.0032,\ 0.0096,\ -0.0032).
\]

So the correct comparison quantity for the full Newton step
\[
\Delta_f:=-J_f^{-1}G_f
\]
is
\[
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
:=
\left\|
\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f
\right\|
\]
or, in normalized form,
\[
\widehat{\mathfrak D}_{\rm Newt}
:=
\frac{\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}}{\|\delta_f\|}.
\]

\section{3. Exact lower bound from the notes}

The uploaded notes already derive
\[
\widehat{\mathfrak D}_{\rm Newt}
\ge
\frac{
\|E_f\|/\|\delta_f\|-\|R_f\|_2/3
}{
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_2+\|R_f\|_2
},
\qquad
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]

Using
\[
\|\delta_f\|\approx 0.03183959798741184,
\qquad
\|E_f\|\ge 0.21427160981790663,
\]
one gets
\[
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|\delta_f\|}
\ge
6.729308620179073.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widehat{\mathfrak D}_{\rm Newt}
\ge
\frac{
6.729308620179073-\|R_f\|_2/3
}{
24.4716494789482+\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\label{eq:newton_barrier_main}
\end{equation}

This is already a sharp quantitative exclusion formula.

\section{4. Strong, very strong, and moderate quartic-dominance regimes}

The notes further evaluate \eqref{eq:newton_barrier_main} in three regimes.

\subsection{Very strong quartic Jacobian dominance}

If
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.1\,\lambda_*
=
0.35212959,
\]
then
\[
\widehat{\mathfrak D}_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.2663713855973499.
\]
Multiplying by
\[
\|\delta_f\|\approx 0.03183959798741184,
\]
gives the absolute barrier
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
\ge
0.008481157832769485.
}
\label{eq:newton_abs_verystrong}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Strong quartic Jacobian dominance}

If
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.2\,\lambda_*
=
0.70425918,
\]
then
\[
\widehat{\mathfrak D}_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.2579834549109606,
\]
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
\ge
0.008214089491768573.
}
\label{eq:newton_abs_strong}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Moderate quartic Jacobian dominance}

If
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.5\,\lambda_*
=
1.76064795,
\]
then
\[
\widehat{\mathfrak D}_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.23417080882818148,
\]
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
\ge
0.007455904413476369.
}
\label{eq:newton_abs_moderate}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Exact meaning of these barriers}

The three bounds \eqref{eq:newton_abs_verystrong}--\eqref{eq:newton_abs_moderate} are not heuristic.
They mean:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_2\le 1.76064795
\Longrightarrow
\left\|
\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f
\right\|
\ge
0.007455904413476369.
}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, if the actual extracted Newton step satisfies
\[
\left\|
\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f
\right\|
<
0.007455904413476369,
\]
then the moderate quartic-dominance regime is impossible.

Likewise,
\[
\left\|
\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f
\right\|
<
0.008214089491768573
\]
rules out strong quartic Jacobian dominance, and
\[
\left\|
\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f
\right\|
<
0.008481157832769485
\]
rules out very strong quartic Jacobian dominance.

Thus the actual six-point Jacobian extraction does not merely decide whether \(R_f\) is small.
It also yields a direct dynamical exclusion test through the observed Newton step.

\section{6. Connection with the currently available admissible prior remainder}

The already available admissible local prior completion satisfies
\[
\|R_f^{\rm prior}\|_2
=
8.081109861025224
>
\lambda_*
\approx
3.5212959.
\]
So the prior admissible completion lies completely outside the small-remainder invertibility-control regime.

Therefore the existing prior evidence and the Newton-barrier theorem point in the same direction:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the corrected residual branch is not naturally consistent with quartic-dominant local control.}
}
\end{equation}

The remaining local structure is instead controlled by a genuinely nontrivial completion beyond the exact four-class quartic baseline.

\section{Final Summary}

The uploaded files still do not contain the actual centered-difference Jacobian
\[
J_f=DG(\delta_f),
\]
but they already contain a stronger quantitative result than a qualitative no-go.

At the corrected seed
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\qquad
\|\delta_f\|\approx 0.03183959798741184,
\]
the quartic Newton predictor is exactly
\[
\Delta_f^{(4)}=-\frac13\delta_f.
\]

Moreover, the vector-level mismatch satisfies
\[
\|E_f\|\ge 0.21427160981790663,
\]
and the exact quartic baseline Jacobian has
\[
\lambda_*=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959,
\qquad
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_2\approx 24.4716494789482.
\]

Therefore the notes already imply the Newton-mismatch barrier
\[
\widehat{\mathfrak D}_{\rm Newt}
\ge
\frac{
6.729308620179073-\|R_f\|_2/3
}{
24.4716494789482+\|R_f\|_2
}.
\]

In particular:

\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.1\lambda_*
\Longrightarrow
\left\|\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f\right\|
\ge
0.008481157832769485,
\]
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.2\lambda_*
\Longrightarrow
\left\|\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f\right\|
\ge
0.008214089491768573,
\]
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.5\lambda_*
\Longrightarrow
\left\|\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f\right\|
\ge
0.007455904413476369.
\]

So the next six-point extraction has an immediate sharp interpretation:

if the actual Newton step comes out closer than \(7.456\times 10^{-3}\) to \(-\delta_f/3\), then even moderate quartic Jacobian dominance is already impossible.

Thus the next frontier is no longer another quartic ansatz.
It is the actual finite extraction of
\[
J_f,\qquad R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\qquad
\Delta_f=-J_f^{-1}G_f,
\]
followed by the Newton-barrier test above.
